<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Planned_missions">Planned missions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Planned missions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6268" data-file-width="10500" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg/290px-Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg/435px-Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg/580px-Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Small_Asteroids_and_Comets_Visited_by_Spacecraft.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Asteroids and comets visited by spacecraft as of 2019 (except Ceres and Vesta), to scale</div></div></div>
<p>Currently, several asteroid-dedicated missions are planned by NASA, JAXA, ESA, and CNSA.
</p><p>NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/Lucy_(spacecraft)" title="Lucy (spacecraft)">Lucy</a></i>, launched in 2021, would visit eight asteroids, one from the <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">main belt</a> and seven <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_trojan" title="Jupiter trojan">Jupiter trojans</a>; it is the first mission to trojans. The main mission would start in 2027.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[140]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[141]</a></sup>
</p><p>In November 2021, NASA launched its <a href="/wiki/Double_Asteroid_Redirection_Test" title="Double Asteroid Redirection Test">Double Asteroid Redirection Test</a> (DART), a mission to test technology for defending Earth against potential hazardous objects. DART will deliberately crash into the <a href="/wiki/Minor-planet_moon" title="Minor-planet moon">minor-planet moon</a> <a href="/wiki/Dimorphos" title="Dimorphos">Dimorphos</a> of the double asteroid <a href="/wiki/65803_Didymos" title="65803 Didymos">Didymos</a> in September 2022 to assess the future potential of a spacecraft impact to deflect an asteroid from a collision course with Earth through a transference of <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">momentum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[142]</a></sup> ESA's <i><a href="/wiki/Hera_(space_mission)" title="Hera (space mission)">Hera</a></i>, planned for launch in 2024, will study the results of the DART impact.  It will measure the size and morphology of the crater, and momentum transmitted by the impact, to determine the efficiency of the deflection produced by DART.
</p><p>NASA's <i><a href="/wiki/Psyche_(spacecraft)" title="Psyche (spacecraft)">Psyche</a></i> would be launched in 2023 or 2024 to study the large metallic asteroid <a href="/wiki/16_Psyche" title="16 Psyche">of the same name</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Janus_(spacecraft)" title="Janus (spacecraft)">Janus</a></i> is a planned dual space probe to be launched as a secondary payload on the <i>Psyche</i> launch.
</p><p>JAXA's <a href="/wiki/DESTINY%2B" title="DESTINY+">DESTINY+</a> is a mission for a flyby of the <a href="/wiki/Geminids" title="Geminids">Geminids</a> meteor shower parent body <a href="/wiki/3200_Phaethon" title="3200 Phaethon">3200 Phaethon</a>, as well as various minor bodies. Its launch is planned for 2024.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dlr-20201112_149-0"><a href="#cite_note-dlr-20201112-149">[143]</a></sup>
</p><p>CNSA's <i><a href="/wiki/Tianwen-2" title="Tianwen-2">Tianwen-2</a></i> is planned to launch in 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[144]</a></sup> It will use <a href="/wiki/Solar_electric_propulsion" title="Solar electric propulsion">solar electric propulsion</a> to explore the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Co-orbital" title="Co-orbital">co-orbital</a> near-Earth asteroid <a href="/wiki/469219_Kamo%CA%BBoalewa" title="469219 Kamoʻoalewa">469219 Kamoʻoalewa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Active_asteroid" title="Active asteroid">active asteroid</a> <a href="/wiki/311P/PanSTARRS" title="311P/PanSTARRS">311P/PanSTARRS</a>. The spacecraft will collect samples of the regolith of Kamo'oalewa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nature20190430_151-0"><a href="#cite_note-nature20190430-151">[145]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_mining">Asteroid mining</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Asteroid mining">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg/220px-Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg/330px-Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg/440px-Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist_Concept_-_Astronaut_Performs_Tethering_Maneuvers_at_Asteroid.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's concept of a crewed mission to an asteroid</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_mining" title="Asteroid mining">Asteroid mining</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_the_asteroids" title="Colonization of the asteroids">Colonization of the asteroids</a></div>
<p>The concept of asteroid mining was proposed in 1970s. Matt Anderson defines successful asteroid mining as "the development of a mining program that is both financially self-sustaining and profitable to its investors".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[146]</a></sup> It has been suggested that asteroids might be used as a source of materials that may be rare or exhausted on Earth,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[147]</a></sup> or materials for constructing <a href="/wiki/Space_habitat" title="Space habitat">space habitats</a>. Materials that are heavy and expensive to launch from Earth may someday be mined from asteroids and used for <a href="/wiki/Space_manufacturing" title="Space manufacturing">space manufacturing</a> and construction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[149]</a></sup>
</p><p>As <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource depletion</a> on Earth becomes more real, the idea of extracting valuable elements from asteroids and returning these to <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> for profit, or using space-based resources to build <a href="/wiki/Space-based_solar_power" title="Space-based solar power">solar-power satellites</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Space_habitats" title="Space habitats">space habitats</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[150]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[151]</a></sup> becomes more attractive. Hypothetically, water processed from ice could refuel orbiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Propellant_depot" title="Propellant depot">propellant depots</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kiss_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kiss-159">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiological</a> perspective, asteroid prospecting could provide scientific data for the search for extraterrestrial intelligence (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SETI" title="SETI">SETI</a>). Some astrophysicists have suggested that if advanced extraterrestrial civilizations employed asteroid mining long ago, the hallmarks of these activities might be detectable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[154]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[156]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mining <a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> is also considered a possibility. As the largest body in the asteroid belt, Ceres could become the main base and transport hub for future asteroid mining infrastructure,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM101_163-0"><a href="#cite_note-AM101-163">[157]</a></sup> allowing mineral resources to be transported to <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, and Earth. Because of its small escape velocity combined with large amounts of water ice, it also could serve as a source of water, fuel, and oxygen for ships going through and beyond the asteroid belt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AM101_163-1"><a href="#cite_note-AM101-163">[157]</a></sup> Transportation from Mars or the Moon to Ceres would be even more energy-efficient than transportation from Earth to the Moon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[158]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threats_to_Earth">Threats to Earth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Threats to Earth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Earth-crossing_minor_planets" title="List of Earth-crossing minor planets">List of Earth-crossing minor planets</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1064" data-file-width="1519" decoding="async" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/220px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/330px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg/440px-SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SmallAsteroidImpacts-Frequency-Bolide-20141114.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Frequency of <a href="/wiki/Bolide" title="Bolide">bolides</a>, small asteroids roughly 1 to 20 meters in diameter impacting Earth's atmosphere</div></div></div>
<p>There is increasing interest in identifying asteroids whose orbits cross <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s, and that could, given enough time, collide with Earth. The three most important groups of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid">near-Earth asteroids</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_asteroid" title="Apollo asteroid">Apollos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amor_asteroid" title="Amor asteroid">Amors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Aten_asteroid" title="Aten asteroid">Atens</a>.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_object" title="Near-Earth object">near-Earth</a> asteroid <a href="/wiki/433_Eros" title="433 Eros">433 Eros</a> had been discovered as long ago as 1898, and the 1930s brought a flurry of similar objects. In order of discovery, these were: <a href="/wiki/1221_Amor" title="1221 Amor">1221 Amor</a>, <a href="/wiki/1862_Apollo" title="1862 Apollo">1862 Apollo</a>, <a href="/wiki/2101_Adonis" title="2101 Adonis">2101 Adonis</a>, and finally <a href="/wiki/69230_Hermes" title="69230 Hermes">69230 Hermes</a>, which approached within 0.005 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> of <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> in 1937. Astronomers began to realize the possibilities of Earth impact.
</p><p>Two events in later decades increased the alarm: the increasing acceptance of the <a href="/wiki/Alvarez_hypothesis" title="Alvarez hypothesis">Alvarez hypothesis</a> that an <a href="/wiki/Impact_event" title="Impact event">impact event</a> resulted in the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction</a>, and the 1994 observation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comet_Shoemaker-Levy_9" title="Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9">Comet Shoemaker-Levy 9</a> crashing into <a href="/wiki/Impact_events_on_Jupiter" title="Impact events on Jupiter">Jupiter</a>. The U.S. military also declassified the information that its <a href="/wiki/Military_satellite" title="Military satellite">military satellites</a>, built to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Detect_nuclear_explosions" title="Detect nuclear explosions">detect nuclear explosions</a>, had detected hundreds of upper-atmosphere impacts by objects ranging from one to ten meters across.
</p><p>All of these considerations helped spur the launch of highly efficient surveys, consisting of charge-coupled device (<a href="/wiki/Charge-coupled_device" title="Charge-coupled device">CCD</a>) cameras and computers directly connected to telescopes. As of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, it was estimated that 89% to 96% of near-Earth asteroids one kilometer or larger in diameter had been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nasa_neo_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-nasa_neo-59">[54]</a></sup> A list of teams using such systems includes:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[160]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Near-Earth_Asteroid_Research" title="Lincoln Near-Earth Asteroid Research">Lincoln Near-Earth Asteroid Research</a> (LINEAR)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_Asteroid_Tracking" title="Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking">Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking</a> (NEAT)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spacewatch" title="Spacewatch">Spacewatch</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/LONEOS" title="LONEOS">Lowell Observatory Near-Earth-Object Search</a> (LONEOS)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Catalina_Sky_Survey" title="Catalina Sky Survey">Catalina Sky Survey</a> (CSS)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pan-STARRS" title="Pan-STARRS">Pan-STARRS</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NEOWISE" title="NEOWISE">NEOWISE</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Asteroid_Terrestrial-impact_Last_Alert_System" title="Asteroid Terrestrial-impact Last Alert System">Asteroid Terrestrial-impact Last Alert System</a> (ATLAS)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Campo_Imperatore_Near-Earth_Object_Survey" title="Campo Imperatore Near-Earth Object Survey">Campo Imperatore Near-Earth Object Survey</a> (CINEOS)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Japanese_Spaceguard_Association" title="Japanese Spaceguard Association">Japanese Spaceguard Association</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Asiago-DLR_Asteroid_Survey" title="Asiago-DLR Asteroid Survey">Asiago-DLR Asteroid Survey</a> (ADAS)</li></ul>
<p>As of 29 October 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the LINEAR system alone had discovered 147,132 asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-167"><a href="#cite_note-167">[161]</a></sup> Among the surveys, 19,266 near-Earth asteroids have been discovered<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-168"><a href="#cite_note-168">[162]</a></sup> including almost 900 more than 1 km (0.6 mi) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>In April 2018, the <a href="/wiki/B612_Foundation" title="B612 Foundation">B612 Foundation</a> reported "It is 100 percent certain we'll be hit [by a devastating asteroid], but we're not 100 percent sure when."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-INQ-20180428_170-0"><a href="#cite_note-INQ-20180428-170">[164]</a></sup> In June 2018, the US <a href="/wiki/National_Science_and_Technology_Council" title="National Science and Technology Council">National Science and Technology Council</a> warned that America is unprepared for an asteroid impact event, and has developed and released the <i>"National Near-Earth Object Preparedness Strategy Action Plan"</i> to better prepare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GIZ-20180621_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-GIZ-20180621-171">[165]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ICARUS-220180522_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-ICARUS-220180522-172">[166]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NYT-20180614_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20180614-173">[167]</a></sup> According to expert testimony in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> in 2013, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> would require at least five years of preparation before a mission to intercept an asteroid could be launched.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US-Congress-20130410_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-US-Congress-20130410-174">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>The United Nations declared 30 June as International <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_Day" title="Asteroid Day">Asteroid Day</a> to educate the public about asteroids. The date of International Asteroid Day commemorates the anniversary of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tunguska_asteroid_impact_over_Siberia" title="Tunguska asteroid impact over Siberia">Tunguska asteroid impact over Siberia</a>, on 30 June 1908.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-175"><a href="#cite_note-175">[169]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176"><a href="#cite_note-176">[170]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chicxulub_impact">Chicxulub impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Chicxulub impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_crater" title="Chicxulub crater">Chicxulub crater</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coast_Impact.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="629" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Coast_Impact.jpg/220px-Coast_Impact.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Coast_Impact.jpg/330px-Coast_Impact.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Coast_Impact.jpg/440px-Coast_Impact.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coast_Impact.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's impression of an asteroid impact on Earth</div></div></div>
<p>The Chicxulub crater is an <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact crater</a> buried underneath the <a href="/wiki/Yucat%C3%A1n_Peninsula" title="Yucatán Peninsula">Yucatán Peninsula</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. Its center is offshore near the communities of <a href="/wiki/Progreso_Municipality,_Yucat%C3%A1n#Communities" title="Progreso Municipality, Yucatán">Chicxulub Puerto</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicxulub_Pueblo" title="Chicxulub Pueblo">Chicxulub Pueblo</a>, after which the crater is named. It was formed when a large asteroid, about 10 kilometers (6.2 miles) in diameter, struck the Earth. The crater is estimated to be 180 kilometers (110 miles) in diameter and 20 kilometers (12 miles) in depth. It is <a href="/wiki/List_of_impact_craters_on_Earth#Largest_craters_(10_Ma_or_more)" title="List of impact craters on Earth">one of the largest confirmed impact structures on Earth</a>, and the only one whose <a href="/wiki/Peak_ring" title="Peak ring">peak ring</a> is intact and directly accessible for scientific research.
</p><p>In the late 1970s, geologist <a href="/wiki/Walter_Alvarez" title="Walter Alvarez">Walter Alvarez</a> and his father, Nobel Prize–winning scientist <a href="/wiki/Luis_Walter_Alvarez" title="Luis Walter Alvarez">Luis Walter Alvarez</a>, put forth their theory that the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction</a> was caused by an impact event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker_2019-03-29_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker_2019-03-29-177">[171]</a></sup> The main evidence of such an impact was contained in a thin layer of clay present in the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_boundary" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene boundary">K–Pg boundary</a> in <a href="/wiki/Gubbio" title="Gubbio">Gubbio, Italy</a>. The Alvarezes and colleagues reported that it contained an <a href="/wiki/Iridium_anomaly" title="Iridium anomaly">abnormally high concentration of iridium</a>, a chemical element rare on earth but common in asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_1979_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez_1979-178">[172]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Becker2002_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-Becker2002-179">[173]</a></sup> Iridium levels in this layer were as much as 160 times above the background level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_et_al-1980_180-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez_et_al-1980-180">[174]</a></sup> It was hypothesized that the iridium was spread into the atmosphere when the impactor was vaporized and settled across the Earth's surface among other material thrown up by the impact, producing the layer of iridium-enriched clay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayell_2005-05-15_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mayell_2005-05-15-181">[175]</a></sup> At the time, consensus was not settled on what caused the Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction and the boundary layer, with theories including a nearby <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernova</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, or a <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_reversal" title="Geomagnetic reversal">geomagnetic reversal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_et_al-1980_180-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez_et_al-1980-180">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 1095">: 1095 </span></sup> The Alvarezes' impact hypothesis was rejected by many paleontologists, who believed that the lack of fossils found close to the K–Pg boundary—the "three-meter problem"—suggested a more gradual die-off of fossil species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newyorker_2019-03-29_177-1"><a href="#cite_note-newyorker_2019-03-29-177">[171]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_2008_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez_2008-182">[176]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is broad consensus that the Chicxulub impactor was an asteroid with a <a href="/wiki/Carbonaceous_chondrite" title="Carbonaceous chondrite">carbonaceous chondrite</a> composition, rather than a comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desch_et_al_2021_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Desch_et_al_2021-183">[177]</a></sup> The impactor was around 10 kilometers (6.2 miles) in diameter<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Desch_et_al_2021_183-1"><a href="#cite_note-Desch_et_al_2021-183">[177]</a></sup>—large enough that, if set at sea level, it would have reached taller than <a href="/wiki/Mount_Everest" title="Mount Everest">Mount Everest</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Alvarez_2008_182-1"><a href="#cite_note-Alvarez_2008-182">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9">: 9 </span></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_deflection_strategies">Asteroid deflection strategies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Asteroid deflection strategies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asteroid_deflection_strategies" title="Asteroid deflection strategies">Asteroid deflection strategies</a> and <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_impact_avoidance" title="Asteroid impact avoidance">Asteroid impact avoidance</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_(Artist%27s_Concept).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg/220px-Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg/330px-Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg/440px-Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_%28Artist%27s_Concept%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spacious_Structure_of_Asteroid_2011_MD_(Artist%27s_Concept).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Artist's concept of spacious structure of near-Earth asteroid <a href="/wiki/2011_MD" title="2011 MD">2011 MD</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184"><a href="#cite_note-184">[178]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Various collision avoidance techniques have different trade-offs with respect to metrics such as overall performance, cost, failure risks, operations, and technology readiness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185"><a href="#cite_note-185">[179]</a></sup> There are various methods for changing the course of an asteroid/comet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HallRoss_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-HallRoss-186">[180]</a></sup> These can be differentiated by various types of attributes such as the type of mitigation (deflection or fragmentation), energy source (kinetic, electromagnetic, gravitational, solar/thermal, or nuclear), and approach strategy (<span class="anchor" id="interception2016-01-26"></span>interception,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[182]</a></sup> rendezvous, or remote station).
</p><p>Strategies fall into two basic sets: fragmentation and delay.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HallRoss_186-1"><a href="#cite_note-HallRoss-186">[180]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-189"><a href="#cite_note-189">[183]</a></sup> Fragmentation concentrates on rendering the impactor harmless by fragmenting it and scattering the fragments so that they miss the Earth or are small enough to burn up in the atmosphere. Delay exploits the fact that both the Earth and the impactor are in orbit. An impact occurs when both reach the same point in space at the same time, or more correctly when some point on Earth's surface intersects the impactor's orbit when the impactor arrives. Since the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> is approximately 12,750 km in diameter and moves at approx. 30 km per second in its orbit, it travels a distance of one planetary diameter in about 425 seconds, or slightly over seven minutes. Delaying, or advancing the impactor's arrival by times of this magnitude can, depending on the exact geometry of the impact, cause it to miss the Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RossParkPorter_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-RossParkPorter-190">[184]</a></sup>
</p><p>"<a href="/wiki/1566_Icarus#Project_Icarus" title="1566 Icarus">Project Icarus</a>" was one of the first projects designed in 1967 as a contingency plan in case of collision with <a href="/wiki/1566_Icarus" title="1566 Icarus">1566 Icarus</a>. The plan relied on the new <a href="/wiki/Saturn_V" title="Saturn V">Saturn V</a> rocket, which did not make its first flight until after the report had been completed. Six Saturn V rockets would be used, each launched at variable intervals from months to hours away from impact. Each rocket was to be fitted with a single 100-megaton <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nuclear_warhead" title="Nuclear warhead">nuclear warhead</a> as well as a modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Service_Module" title="Apollo Service Module">Apollo Service Module</a> and uncrewed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apollo_Command_Module" title="Apollo Command Module">Apollo Command Module</a> for guidance to the target. The warheads would be detonated 30 meters from the surface, deflecting or partially destroying the asteroid. Depending on the subsequent impacts on the course or the destruction of the asteroid, later missions would be modified or cancelled as needed. The "last-ditch" launch of the sixth rocket would be 18 hours prior to impact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Portree_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Portree-191">[185]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fiction">Fiction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Fiction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroids_in_fiction" title="Asteroids in fiction">Asteroids in fiction</a></div>
<p>Asteroids and the asteroid belt are a staple of science fiction stories. Asteroids play several potential roles in science fiction: as places human beings might colonize, resources for extracting minerals, hazards encountered by spacecraft traveling between two other points, and as a threat to life on Earth or other inhabited planets, dwarf planets, and natural satellites by potential impact.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Asteroid&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-columns references-column-width reflist-lower-alpha">

</li>

</li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For example, the <i>Annual of Scientific Discovery</i>: "Professor J. Watson has been awarded by the Paris Academy of Sciences, the astronomical prize, Lalande foundation, for the discovery of eight new asteroids in one year. The planet <a href="/wiki/110_Lydia" title="110 Lydia">Lydia</a> (No. 110), discovered by M. Borelly at the Marseilles Observatory [...] M. Borelly had previously discovered two planets bearing the numbers 91 and 99 in the system of asteroids revolving between Mars and Jupiter".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[27]</a></sup><br/>The <i>Universal English Dictionary</i> (John Craig, 1869) lists the asteroids (and gives their pronunciations) up to <a href="/wiki/64_Angelina" title="64 Angelina">64 Angelina</a>, along with the definition "one of the recently-discovered planets." At this time it was common to anglicize the spellings of the names, e.g. "Aglaia" for <a href="/wiki/47_Aglaja" title="47 Aglaja">47 Aglaja</a> and "Atalanta" for <a href="/wiki/36_Atalante" title="36 Atalante">36 Atalante</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-42"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-42">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, a joint <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JPL" title="JPL">JPL</a> public-outreach website states: <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>We include Trojans (bodies captured in Jupiter's 4th and 5th Lagrange points), Centaurs (bodies in orbit between Jupiter and Neptune), and trans-Neptunian objects (orbiting beyond Neptune) in our definition of "asteroid" as used on this site, even though they may more correctly be called "minor planets" instead of asteroids.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[37]</a></sup></p></blockquote></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The definition in the 1995 paper (Beech and Steel) has been updated by a 2010 paper (Rubin and Grossman) and the discovery of 1 meter asteroids.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>

<li id="cite_note-planetary-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-planetary_85-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-planetary_85-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1"><a class="external text" href="http://www.planetary.org/explore/topics/asteroids_and_comets/ceres.html" rel="nofollow">"Asteroid 1 Ceres"</a>. The Planetary Society. <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070929092440/http://planetary.org/explore/topics/asteroids_and_comets/ceres.html" rel="nofollow">Archived</a> from the original on 29 September 2007<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 October</span> 2007</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=Asteroid+1+Ceres&amp;rft.pub=The+Planetary+Society&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.planetary.org%2Fexplore%2Ftopics%2Fasteroids_and_comets%2Fceres.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAsteroid"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-satellites-86"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-satellites_86-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-satellites_86-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFMcFaddenSkillmanMemarsadeghi2018">McFadden, Lucy A.; Skillman, David R.; Memarsadeghi, N (December 2018). "Dawn mission's search for satellites of Ceres: Intact protoplanets don't have satellites". <i>Icarus</i>. <b>316</b>: 191–204. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bibcode_(identifier)" title="Bibcode (identifier)">Bibcode</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://ui.adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2018Icar..316..191M" rel="nofollow">2018Icar..316..191M</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1016%2Fj.icarus.2018.02.017" rel="nofollow">10.1016/j.icarus.2018.02.017</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/S2CID_(identifier)" title="S2CID (identifier)">S2CID</a> <a class="external text" href="https://api.semanticscholar.org/CorpusID:125181684" rel="nofollow">125181684</a>. <q>Examination of the physical properties of the 41 largest and most massive main belt asteroids suggests that large asteroids without satellites are intact and their interiors have internal strength. This is consistent with results from the Dawn mission at both Vesta and Ceres. Ceres' volatile-rich composition also is a likely contributor to both the absence of satellites at Ceres and of Ceres meteorites at Earth. These results suggest that collisional disruption creating rubble pile structure is a necessary condition for formation of satellites around main belt asteroids.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Icarus&amp;rft.atitle=Dawn+mission%27s+search+for+satellites+of+Ceres%3A+Intact+protoplanets+don%27t+have+satellites&amp;rft.volume=316&amp;rft.pages=191-204&amp;rft.date=2018-12&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fapi.semanticscholar.org%2FCorpusID%3A125181684%23id-name%3DS2CID&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1016%2Fj.icarus.2018.02.017&amp;rft_id=info%3Abibcode%2F2018Icar..316..191M&amp;rft.aulast=McFadden&amp;rft.aufirst=Lucy+A.&amp;rft.au=Skillman%2C+David+R.&amp;rft.au=Memarsadeghi%2C+N&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AAsteroid"></span></span>
</li>
